<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Metal–organic framework</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_11637_Scanning_electron_microscope_image_of_the_seed_inside_the_MOF_crystals.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1967" data-file-width="2902" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/CSIRO_ScienceImage_11637_Scanning_electron_microscope_image_of_the_seed_inside_the_MOF_crystals.jpg/220px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_11637_Scanning_electron_microscope_image_of_the_seed_inside_the_MOF_crystals.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/CSIRO_ScienceImage_11637_Scanning_electron_microscope_image_of_the_seed_inside_the_MOF_crystals.jpg/330px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_11637_Scanning_electron_microscope_image_of_the_seed_inside_the_MOF_crystals.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/CSIRO_ScienceImage_11637_Scanning_electron_microscope_image_of_the_seed_inside_the_MOF_crystals.jpg/440px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_11637_Scanning_electron_microscope_image_of_the_seed_inside_the_MOF_crystals.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_11637_Scanning_electron_microscope_image_of_the_seed_inside_the_MOF_crystals.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scanning electron microscope image of the seed inside a MOF crystal</div></div></div>
<p><b>Metal–organic frameworks</b> (MOFs) are compounds consisting of <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">ions</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cluster_compound" title="Cluster compound">clusters</a> coordinated to <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligands" title="Ligands">ligands</a> to form one-, two-, or three-dimensional structures.  They are a subclass of <a href="/wiki/Coordination_polymer" title="Coordination polymer">coordination polymers</a>, with the special feature that they are often <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porous" title="Porous">porous</a>. The organic ligands included are sometimes referred to as "struts", one example being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1,4-benzenedicarboxylic_acid" title="1,4-benzenedicarboxylic acid">1,4-benzenedicarboxylic acid</a> (BDC).
</p><p>More formally, a metal–organic framework is a coordination network with organic <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a> containing potential voids. A coordination network is a coordination compound extending, through repeating coordination entities, in one dimension, but with cross-links between two or more individual chains, loops, or spiro-links, or a coordination compound extending through repeating coordination entities in two or three dimensions; and finally a <a href="/wiki/Coordination_polymer" title="Coordination polymer">coordination polymer</a> is a coordination compound with repeating coordination entities extending in one, two, or three dimensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Batten13_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Batten13-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>In some cases, the pores are stable during elimination of the guest molecules (often solvents) and could be refilled with other compounds.  Because of this property, MOFs are of interest for the storage of gases such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>. Other possible applications of MOFs are in <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> purification, in <a href="/wiki/Gas_separation" title="Gas separation">gas separation</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a>, as sensors and as <a href="/wiki/Supercapacitor" title="Supercapacitor">supercapacitors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The synthesis and properties of MOFs constitute the primary focus of the discipline called <em>reticular chemistry</em> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <em>reticulum</em>, "small net").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OKeeffeAndYaghi_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-OKeeffeAndYaghi-3">[3]</a></sup> Differently from MOFs <a href="/wiki/Covalent_organic_framework" title="Covalent organic framework">covalent organic framework</a> (COFs) are made entirely from light elements (H, B, C, N, and O) with extended structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>MOFs are composed of two major components: a metal ion or cluster of metal ions and an organic molecule called a linker. For this reason, the materials are often referred to as hybrid organic–inorganic materials; however, this terminology has recently been explicitly discouraged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Batten13_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Batten13-1">[1]</a></sup> The organic units are typically <a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">mono-, di-, tri-, or tetravalent</a> ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czaja09_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Czaja09-5">[5]</a></sup> The choice of metal and linker dictates the structure and hence properties of the MOF. For example, the metal's <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination</a> preference influences the size and shape of pores by dictating how many ligands can bind to the metal and in which orientation.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Classification of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hybrid_materials" title="Hybrid materials">hybrid materials</a> based on dimensionality <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</caption>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>To describe and organize the structures of MOFs, a system of nomenclature has been developed. Subunits of a MOF, called secondary building units (SBU), can be described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topologies" title="Topologies">topologies</a> common to several structures. Each topology, also called a net, is assigned a symbol, consisting of three lower-case letters in bold. <a href="/wiki/MOF-5" title="MOF-5">MOF-5</a>, for example, has a <b>pcu</b> net. The database of net structures can be found at the <a class="external text" href="http://rcsr.anu.edu.au/" rel="nofollow">Reticular Chemistry Structure Resource</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reviews_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reviews-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Attached to the SBUs are <a href="/wiki/Bridging_ligand" title="Bridging ligand">bridging ligands</a>.  For MOF's, typical bridging ligands are  di- and tricarboxylic acids.  These ligands typically have rigid backbones.  Examples are benzene-1,4-dicarboxylic acid (BDC or teraphthalic acid, biphenyl-4,4'-dicarboxylic acid (BPDC), and the tricarboxylic acid <a href="/wiki/Trimesic_acid" title="Trimesic acid">trimesic acid</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BasicZnAcetate.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="526" data-file-width="633" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/BasicZnAcetate.png/200px-BasicZnAcetate.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/BasicZnAcetate.png/300px-BasicZnAcetate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/BasicZnAcetate.png/400px-BasicZnAcetate.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BasicZnAcetate.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SBU's are often derived from basic zinc acetate structure, the acetates being replaced by rigid di- and tricarboxylates.</div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Synthesis">Synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_Synthesis">General Synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: General Synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The study of MOFs developed from the study of <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolites</a>, except for the use of preformed ligands. MOFs and zeolites are produced almost exclusively by <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_synthesis" title="Hydrothermal synthesis">hydrothermal</a> or solvothermal techniques, where crystals are slowly grown from a hot solution. In contrast with zeolites, MOFs are constructed from bridging organic ligands that remain intact throughout the synthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheetham99_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cheetham99-8">[8]</a></sup> Zeolite synthesis often makes use of a "template". Templates are ions that influence the structure of the growing inorganic framework.  Typical templating ions are quaternary ammonium cations, which are removed later. In MOFs, the framework is templated by the SBU (secondary building unit) and the organic ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bucar07_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bucar07-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parnham07_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parnham07-10">[10]</a></sup> A templating approach that is useful for MOFs intended for gas storage is the use of metal-binding solvents such as N,N-diethylformamide and water. In these cases, metal sites are exposed when the solvent is evacuated, allowing hydrogen to bind at these sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dinca08_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dinca08-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since ligands in MOFs typically bind reversibly, the slow growth of crystals often allows defects to be redissolved, resulting in a material with millimeter-scale crystals and a near-equilibrium defect density. Solvothermal synthesis is useful for growing crystals suitable to structure determination, because crystals grow over the course of hours to days. However, the use of MOFs as storage materials for consumer products demands an immense scale-up of their synthesis. Scale-up of MOFs has not been widely studied, though several groups have demonstrated that microwaves can be used to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleate" title="Nucleate">nucleate</a> MOF crystals rapidly from solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ni06_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ni06-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi08_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Choi08-13">[13]</a></sup> This technique, termed "microwave-assisted solvothermal synthesis", is widely used in the zeolite literature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cheetham99_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cheetham99-8">[8]</a></sup> and produces micron-scale crystals in a matter of seconds to minutes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ni06_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ni06-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Choi08_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Choi08-13">[13]</a></sup> in yields similar to the slow growth methods.
</p><p>A solvent-free synthesis of a range of crystalline MOFs has been described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Usually the metal acetate and the organic proligand are mixed and ground up with a ball mill. Cu<sub>3</sub>(BTC)<sub>2</sub> can be quickly synthesised in this way in quantitative yield. In the case of Cu<sub>3</sub>(BTC)<sub>2</sub> the morphology of the solvent free synthesised product was the same as the industrially made Basolite C300. It is thought that localised melting of the components due to the high collision energy in the ball mill may assist the reaction. The formation of acetic acid as a by-product in the reactions in the ball mill may also help in the reaction having a solvent effect<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> in the ball mill.
</p><p>A recent advancement in the solvent-free preparation of MOF films and composites is their synthesis by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_vapor_deposition" title="Chemical vapor deposition">chemical vapor deposition</a>. This process, MOF-CVD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
was first demonstrated for ZIF-8 and consist of two steps. In a first step, metal oxide precursor layers are deposited. In the second step, these precursor layers are exposed to <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimed</a> ligand molecules, that induce a phase transformation to the MOF crystal lattice. Formation of water during this reaction plays a crucial role in directing the transformation.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-synthetic_Modification">Post-synthetic Modification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Post-synthetic Modification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the three-dimensional structure and internal environment of the pores can be in theory controlled through proper selection of nodes and organic linking groups, the direct synthesis of such materials with the desired functionalities can be difficult due to the high sensitivity of MOF systems. Thermal and chemical sensitivity, as well as high reactivity of reaction materials, can make forming desired products challenging to achieve. The exchange of guest molecules and <a href="/wiki/Counterion" title="Counterion">counter-ions</a> and the removal of solvents allow for some additional functionality but are still limited to the integral parts of the framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-17">[17]</a></sup> The post-synthetic exchange of organic linkers and metal ions is an expanding area of the field and opens up possibilities for more complex structures, increased functionality, and greater system control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ligand_Exchange">Ligand Exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ligand Exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Post-synthetic modification techniques can be used to exchange an existing organic linking group in a prefabricated MOF with a new linker by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligand_exchange" title="Ligand exchange">ligand exchange</a> or partial ligand exchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-19">[19]</a></sup> This exchange allows for the pores and, in some cases the overall framework of MOFs, to be tailored for specific purposes. Some of these uses include fine-tuning the material for selective adsorption, gas storage, and catalysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dinca08_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dinca08-11">[11]</a></sup> To perform ligand exchange prefabricated MOF crystals are washed with solvent and then soaked in a solution of the new linker. The exchange often requires heat and occurs on the time scale of a few days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-19">[19]</a></sup> Post-synthetic ligand exchange also enables the incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional groups</a> into MOFs that otherwise would not survive MOF synthesis, due to temperature, pH, or other reaction conditions, or hinder the synthesis itself by competition with donor groups on the loaning ligand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_Exchange">Metal Exchange</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Metal Exchange">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Post-synthetic modification techniques can also be used to exchange an existing metal ion in a prefabricated MOF with a new metal ion by metal ion exchange . The complete metal metathesis from an integral part of the framework has been achieved without altering the framework or pore structure of the MOF. Similarly to post-synthetic ligand exchange post-synthetic metal exchange is performed by washing prefabricated MOF crystals with solvent and then soaking the crystal in a solution of the new metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Post-synthetic metal exchange allows for a simple route to the formation of MOFs with the same framework yet different metal ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stratified_Synthesis">Stratified Synthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Stratified Synthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In addition to modifying the functionality of the ligands and metals themselves, post-synthetic modification can be used to expand upon the structure of the MOF. Using post-synthetic modification MOFs can be converted from a highly ordered crystalline material toward a heterogeneous porous material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Using post-synthetic techniques, it is possible for the controlled installation of domains within a MOF crystal which exhibit unique structural and functional characteristics. Core-shell MOFs and other layered MOFs have been prepared where layers of have unique functionalization but in most cases are <a href="/wiki/Crystallography" title="Crystallography">crystallographically</a> compatible from layer to layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composite_materials">Composite materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Composite materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Another approach to increasing adsorption in MOFs is to alter the system in such a way that <a href="/wiki/Chemisorption" title="Chemisorption">chemisorption</a> becomes possible. This functionality has been introduced by making a composite material, which contains a MOF and a complex of <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> with <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a>. In an effect known as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_spillover" title="Hydrogen spillover">hydrogen spillover</a>, H<sub>2</sub> can bind to the platinum surface through a dissociative mechanism which cleaves the hydrogen molecule into two hydrogen atoms and enables them to travel down the activated carbon onto the surface of the MOF. This innovation produced a threefold increase in the room-temperature storage capacity of a MOF; however, desorption can take upwards of 12 hours, and reversible desorption is sometimes observed for only two cycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li07_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li07-24">[24]</a></sup> The relationship between hydrogen spillover and hydrogen storage properties in MOFs is not well understood but may prove relevant to hydrogen storage.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_storage">Hydrogen storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hydrogen storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">Hydrogen storage</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">Molecular</a> hydrogen has the highest <a href="/wiki/Specific_energy" title="Specific energy">specific energy</a> of any fuel. However unless the hydrogen gas is compressed, its volumetric energy density is very low, so the transportation and storage of hydrogen require energy-intensive compression and liquefaction processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H2Economy_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-H2Economy-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Therefore, development of new hydrogen storage methods which decrease the concomitant pressure required for practical volumetric energy density is an active area of research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H2Economy_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-H2Economy-25">[25]</a></sup> MOFs attract attention as materials for adsorptive hydrogen storage because of their high <a href="/wiki/Specific_surface_area" title="Specific surface area">specific surface areas</a> and surface to volume ratios, as well as their chemically tunable structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup> Compared to an empty <a href="/wiki/Gas_cylinder" title="Gas cylinder">gas cylinder</a>, a MOF-filled gas cylinder can store more hydrogen at a given pressure because hydrogen molecules <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adsorb" title="Adsorb">adsorb</a> to the surface of MOFs. Furthermore, MOFs are free of dead-volume, so there is almost no loss of storage capacity as a result of space-blocking by non-accessible volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czaja09_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Czaja09-5">[5]</a></sup> Also, because the hydrogen uptake is based primarily on <a href="/wiki/Physisorption" title="Physisorption">physisorption</a>, many MOFs have a fully reversible uptake-and-release behavior. No large <a href="/wiki/Activation_energy" title="Activation energy">activation barriers</a> are required when liberating the adsorbed hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czaja09_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Czaja09-5">[5]</a></sup> The storage capacity of a MOF is limited by the liquid-phase density of hydrogen because the benefits provided by MOFs can be realized only if the hydrogen is in its gaseous state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czaja09_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Czaja09-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The extent to which a gas can adsorb to a MOF's surface depends on the temperature and pressure of the gas. In general, adsorption increases with decreasing temperature and increasing pressure (until a maximum is reached, typically 20-30 bar, after which the adsorption capacity decreases).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czaja09_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Czaja09-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup> However, MOFs to be used for <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_storage" title="Hydrogen storage">hydrogen storage</a> in automotive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_cells" title="Fuel cells">fuel cells</a> need to operate efficiently at ambient temperature and pressures between 1 and 100 bar, as these are the values that are deemed safe for automotive applications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MOF-177.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="757" data-file-width="1248" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/MOF-177.tif/lossy-page1-220px-MOF-177.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/MOF-177.tif/lossy-page1-330px-MOF-177.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/MOF-177.tif/lossy-page1-440px-MOF-177.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MOF-177.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>MOF-177</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">U.S. Department of Energy</a> (DOE) has published a list of yearly technical system targets for on-board hydrogen storage for light-duty fuel cell vehicles which guide researchers in the field (5.5 wt %/40 g L<sup>−1</sup> by 2017; 7.5 wt %/70 g L<sup>−1</sup> ultimate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Materials with high porosity and high surface area such as MOFs have been designed and synthesized in an effort to meet these targets. These adsorptive materials generally work via physical adsorption rather than chemisorption due to the large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HOMO-LUMO" title="HOMO-LUMO">HOMO-LUMO</a> gap and low HOMO energy level of molecular hydrogen. A benchmark material to this end is MOF-177 which was found to store hydrogen at 7.5 wt % with a volumetric capacity of 32 g L<sup>−1</sup> at 77 K and 70 bar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> MOF-177 consists of [Zn<sub>4</sub>O]<sup>6+</sup> clusters interconnected by 1,3,5-benzebetribenzoate organic linkers and has a measured <a href="/wiki/BET_theory" title="BET theory">BET</a> surface area of 4630 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. Another exemplary material is PCN-61 which exhibits a hydrogen uptake of 6.24 wt % and 42.5 g L<sup>−1</sup> at 35 bar and 77 K and 2.25 wt % at atmospheric pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> PCN-61 consists of [Cu<sub>2</sub>]<sup>4+</sup> paddle-wheel units connected through 5,5′,5′′-benzene-1,3,5-triyltris(1-ethynyl-2-isophthalate) organic linkers and has a measured BET surface area of 3000 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. Despite these promising MOF examples, the classes of synthetic porous materials with the highest performance for practical hydrogen storage are <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent_organic_frameworks" title="Covalent organic frameworks">covalent organic frameworks</a> (COFs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_principles">Design principles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Design principles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Practical applications of MOFs for hydrogen storage are met with several challenges. For hydrogen adsorption near room temperature, the hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> would need to be increased considerably.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup> Several classes of MOFs have been explored, including <a href="/wiki/Carboxylate" title="Carboxylate">carboxylate</a>-based MOFs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterocyclic" title="Heterocyclic">heterocyclic</a> <a href="/wiki/Azole" title="Azole">azolate</a>-based MOFs, metal-cyanide MOFs, and <a href="/wiki/Covalent_organic_framework" title="Covalent organic framework">covalent organic frameworks</a>. Carboxylate-based MOFs have by far received the most attention because
</p>
<ol><li>they are either commercially available or easily synthesized,</li>
<li>they have high acidity (pK<sub>a</sub> ˜ 4) allowing for facile <i><a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">in situ</a></i> deprotonation,</li>
<li>the metal-carboxylate bond formation is reversible, facilitating the formation of well-ordered crystalline MOFs, and</li>
<li>the bridging <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bidentate" title="Bidentate">bidentate</a> coordination ability of carboxylate groups favors the high degree of framework connectivity and strong metal-ligand bonds necessary to maintain MOF architecture under the conditions required to evacuate the solvent from the pores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>The most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transition_metals" title="Transition metals">transition metals</a> employed in carboxylate-based frameworks are Cu<sup>2+</sup> and Zn<sup>2+</sup>. Lighter <a href="/wiki/Main-group_element" title="Main-group element">main group</a> metal ions have also been explored. Be<sub>12</sub>(OH)<sub>12</sub>(BTB)<sub>4</sub>, the first successfully synthesized and structurally characterized MOF consisting of a light main group metal ion, shows high hydrogen storage capacity, but it is too toxic to be employed practically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sumida09_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sumida09-31">[31]</a></sup> There is considerable effort being put forth in developing MOFs composed of other light main group metal ions, such as magnesium in Mg<sub>4</sub>(BDC)<sub>3</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following is a list of several MOFs that are considered to have the best properties for hydrogen storage as of May 2012 (in order of decreasing hydrogen storage capacity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup> While each MOF described has its advantages, none of these MOFs reach all of the standards set by the U.S. DOE. Therefore, it is not yet known whether materials with high surface areas, small pores, or di- or trivalent metal clusters produce the most favorable MOFs for hydrogen storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czaja09_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Czaja09-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p><br/>
<b>Zn<sub>4</sub>O(BTE)(BPDC)</b>, where BTE<sup>3−</sup>=4,4′,4″-[benzene-1,3,5-triyl-tris(ethyne-2,1-diyl)]tribenzoate and BPDC<sup>2−</sup>=biphenyl-4,4′-dicarboxylate (MOF-210) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furukawa_Science_2010_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Furukawa_Science_2010-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Hydrogen storage capacity (at 77 K):</i> 8.6 excess wt% (17.6  total wt%) at 77 K and 80 bar. 44 total g H<sub>2</sub>/L at 80 bar and 77 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furukawa_Science_2010_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Furukawa_Science_2010-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Hydrogen storage capacity (at 298 K):</i> 2.90 delivery wt% (1-100 bar) at 298 K and 100 bar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mendoza_COFs_MOFs_2012_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-mendoza_COFs_MOFs_2012-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Zn<sub>4</sub>O(BBC)<sub>2</sub></b>, where BBC<sup>3−</sup>=4,4′,4″-[benzene-1,3,5-triyl-tris(benzene-4,1-diyl)]tribenzoate (MOF-200) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furukawa_Science_2010_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Furukawa_Science_2010-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Hydrogen storage capacity (at 77 K):</i> 7.4 excess wt% (16.3  total wt%) at 77 K and 80 bar. 36 total g H<sub>2</sub>/L at 80 bar and 77 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furukawa_Science_2010_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Furukawa_Science_2010-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Hydrogen storage capacity (at 298 K):</i> 3.24 delivery wt% (1-100 bar) at 298 K and 100 bar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mendoza_COFs_MOFs_2012_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-mendoza_COFs_MOFs_2012-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Zn<sub>4</sub>O(BTB)<sub>2</sub></b>, where BTB<sup>3−</sup>=1,3,5-benzenetribenzoate (MOF-177) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowsell04_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rowsell04-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Structure:</i> Tetrahedral [Zn<sub>4</sub>O]<sup>6+</sup> units are linked by large, triangular tricarboxylate ligands. Six diamond-shaped channels (upper) with diameter of 10.8 Å surround a pore containing eclipsed BTB<sup>3−</sup> <a href="/wiki/Moiety_(chemistry)" title="Moiety (chemistry)">moieties</a> (lower).
</p><p><i>Hydrogen storage capacity:</i> 7.1 wt% at 77 K and 40 bar; 11.4 wt% at 78 bar and 77 K.
</p><p>MOF-177 has larger pores, so hydrogen is compressed within holes rather than adsorbed to the surface. This leads to higher total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravimetric" title="Gravimetric">gravimetric</a> uptake but lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volumetric" title="Volumetric">volumetric</a> storage density compared to MOF-5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-8"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Zn<sub>4</sub>O(BDC)<sub>3</sub></b>, where BDC<sup>2−</sup>=1,4-benzenedicarboxylate (MOF-5) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosi03_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosi03-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Structure:</i> Square openings are either 13.8 or 9.2 Å depending on the orientation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic" title="Aromatic">aromatic</a> rings.
</p><p><i>Hydrogen storage capacity:</i> 7.1 wt% at 77 K and 40 bar ; 10 wt% at 100 bar; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volumetric" title="Volumetric">volumetric</a> storage density of 66 g/L.
</p><p>MOF-5 has received much attention from theorists because of the partial charges on the MOF surface, which provide a means of strengthening the binding hydrogen through <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a>-induced intermolecular interactions; however, MOF-5 has poor performance at room temperature (9.1 g/L at 100 bar).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-9"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Mn<sub>3</sub>[(Mn<sub>4</sub>Cl)<sub>3</sub>(BTT)<sub>8</sub>]<sub>2</sub></b>, where H<sub>3</sub>BTT=benzene-1,3,5-tris(1<i>H</i>-tetrazole) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dinca06_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dinca06-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Structure:</i> Consists of truncated octahedral cages that share square faces, leading to pores of about 10 Å in diameter. Contains open Mn<sup>2+</sup> coordination sites.
</p><p><i>Hydrogen storage capacity:</i> 60 g/L at 77 K and 90 bar; 12.1 g/L at 90 bar and 298 K.
</p><p>This MOF is the first demonstration of open metal coordination sites increasing strength of hydrogen adsorption, which results in improved performance at 298 K. It has relatively strong metal-hydrogen interactions, attributed to a <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin state</a> change upon binding or to a classical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coulombic_attraction" title="Coulombic attraction">Coulombic attraction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-10"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Cu<sub>3</sub>(BTC)<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub></b>, where H<sub>3</sub>BTC=<b>1,3,5-benzenetricarboxylic acid</b> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lee05_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lee05-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Structure:</i> Consists of octahedral cages that share paddlewheel units to define pores of about 9.8 Å in diameter.
</p><p>High hydrogen uptake is attributed to overlapping attractive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potentials" title="Potentials">potentials</a> from multiple copper paddle-wheel units: each Cu(II) center can potentially lose a terminal solvent <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> bound in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_of_rotation" title="Axis of rotation">axial</a> position, providing an open coordination site for hydrogen binding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-11"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_impacts_on_hydrogen_storage_capacity">Structural impacts on hydrogen storage capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Structural impacts on hydrogen storage capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To date, hydrogen storage in MOFs at room temperature is a battle between maximizing storage capacity and maintaining reasonable desorption rates, while conserving the integrity of the adsorbent framework (e.g. completely evacuating pores, preserving the MOF structure, etc.) over many cycles. There are two major strategies governing the design of MOFs for hydrogen storage:
</p>
<dl><dd>1) to increase the theoretical storage capacity of the material, and</dd>
<dd>2) to bring the operating conditions closer to ambient temperature and pressure. Rowsell and <a href="/wiki/Omar_M._Yaghi" title="Omar M. Yaghi">Yaghi</a> have identified several directions to these ends in some of the early papers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowsell05_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rowsell05-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowsell06_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rowsell06-39">[39]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_area">Surface area</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Surface area">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The general trend in MOFs used for hydrogen storage is that the greater the surface area, the more hydrogen the MOF can store. High surface area materials tend to exhibit increased micropore volume and inherently low bulk density, allowing for more hydrogen adsorption to occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-12"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_adsorption_enthalpy">Hydrogen adsorption enthalpy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Hydrogen adsorption enthalpy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>High hydrogen adsorption <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">enthalpy</a> is also important. Theoretical studies have shown that 22-25 kJ/mol interactions are ideal for hydrogen storage at room temperature, as they are strong enough to adsorb H<sub>2</sub>, but weak enough to allow for quick desorption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrone08_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garrone08-40">[40]</a></sup> The interaction between hydrogen and uncharged organic linkers is not this strong, and so a considerable amount of work has gone in synthesis of MOFs with exposed metal sites, to which hydrogen adsorbs with an enthalpy of 5-10 kJ/mol. Synthetically, this may be achieved by using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligands" title="Ligands">ligands</a> whose geometries prevent the metal from being fully coordinated, by removing <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(chemistry)" title="Volatility (chemistry)">volatile</a> metal-bound solvent molecules over the course of synthesis, and by post-synthetic impregnation with additional metal cations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dinca08_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dinca08-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dinca06_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dinca06-36">[36]</a></sup> (C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>5</sub>)V(CO)<sub>3</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>) and Mo(CO)<sub>5</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>) are great examples of increased binding energy due to open metal coordination sites;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kubas01_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kubas01-41">[41]</a></sup> however, their high metal-hydrogen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bond_dissociation_energies" title="Bond dissociation energies">bond dissociation energies</a> result in a tremendous release of heat upon loading with hydrogen, which is not favorable for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel_cells" title="Fuel cells">fuel cells</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-13"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup> MOFs, therefore, should avoid orbital interactions that lead to such strong metal-hydrogen bonds and employ simple charge-induced <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a> interactions, as demonstrated in Mn<sub>3</sub>[(Mn<sub>4</sub>Cl)<sub>3</sub>(BTT)<sub>8</sub>]<sub>2</sub>.
</p><p>An association energy of 22-25 kJ/mol is typical of charge-induced dipole interactions, and so there is interest in the use of charged linkers and metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-14"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup> The metal–hydrogen bond strength is diminished in MOFs, probably due to charge diffusion, so 2+ and 3+ metal ions are being studied to strengthen this interaction even further. A problem with this approach is that MOFs with exposed metal surfaces have lower concentrations of linkers; this makes them difficult to synthesize, as they are prone to framework collapse. This may diminish their useful lifetimes as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dinca08_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dinca08-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-15"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup> Most common strategies to increase this binding energy for MOFs and molecular hydrogen have been reviewed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H2_MOFs_review_2009_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-H2_MOFs_review_2009-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sensitivity_to_airborne_moisture">Sensitivity to airborne moisture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sensitivity to airborne moisture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>MOFs are frequently sensitive to moisture in the air. In particular, IRMOF-1 degrades in the presence of small amounts of water at room temperature. Studies on metal analogues have unraveled the ability of metals other than Zn to stand higher water concentrations at high temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellarosa12_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellarosa12-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellarosa12b_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellarosa12b-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>To compensate for this, specially constructed storage containers are required, which can be costly. Strong metal-ligand bonds, such as in metal-imidazolate, -triazolate, and -pyrazolate frameworks, are known to decrease a MOF's sensitivity to air, reducing the expense of storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-16"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pore_size">Pore size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Pore size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In a microporous material where <a href="/wiki/Physisorption" title="Physisorption">physisorption</a> and weak <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_forces" title="Van der Waals forces">van der Waals forces</a> dominate adsorption, the storage density is greatly dependent on the size of the pores. Calculations of idealized homogeneous materials, such as graphitic carbons and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotubes</a>, predict that a microporous material with 7 Å-wide pores will exhibit maximum hydrogen uptake at room temperature. At this width, exactly two layers of hydrogen molecules adsorb on opposing surfaces with no space left in between.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-17"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stern_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-stern-45">[45]</a></sup>
10 Å-wide pores are also of ideal size because at this width, exactly three layers of hydrogen can exist with no space in between.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-18"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup> (A hydrogen molecule has a bond length of 0.74 Å with a van der Waals radius of 1.17 Å for each atom; therefore, its effective van der Waals length is 3.08 Å.) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dolgonos05_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dolgonos05-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_defects">Structural defects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Structural defects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Structural defects also play an important role in the performance of MOFs. Room-temperature hydrogen uptake via bridged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adsorption_spillover" title="Adsorption spillover">spillover</a> is mainly governed by structural defects, which can have two effects:
</p>
<dl><dd>1) a partially collapsed framework can block access to pores; thereby reducing hydrogen uptake, and</dd>
<dd>2) lattice defects can create an intricate array of new pores and channels causing increased hydrogen uptake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsao09_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsao09-47">[47]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Structural defects can also leave metal-containing nodes incompletely coordinated. This enhances the performance of MOFs used for hydrogen storage by increasing the number of accessible metal centers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mulfort09_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mulfort09-48">[48]</a></sup> Finally, structural defects can affect the transport of <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">phonons</a>, which affects the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> of the MOF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huang07_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huang07-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_adsorption">Hydrogen adsorption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hydrogen adsorption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">Adsorption</a> is the process of trapping atoms or molecules that are incident on a surface; therefore the adsorption capacity of a material increases with its surface area. In three dimensions, the maximum surface area will be obtained by a structure which is highly porous, such that atoms and molecules can access internal surfaces. This simple qualitative argument suggests that the highly porous metal-organic frameworks (MOFs) should be excellent candidates for hydrogen storage devices.
</p><p>Adsorption can be broadly classified as being one of two types: <a href="/wiki/Physisorption" title="Physisorption">physisorption</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chemisorption" title="Chemisorption">chemisorption</a>. Physisorption is characterized by weak <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_interactions" title="Van der Waals interactions">van der Waals interactions</a>, and bond enthalpies typically less than 20 kJ/mol. Chemisorption, alternatively, is defined by stronger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Covalent" title="Covalent">covalent</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_bond" title="Ionic bond">ionic bonds</a>, with bond enthalpies between 250 and 500 kJ/mol. In both cases, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adsorbate" title="Adsorbate">adsorbate</a> atoms or molecules (i.e. the particles which adhere to the surface) are attracted to the adsorbent (solid) surface because of the surface energy that results from unoccupied bonding locations at the surface. The degree of <a href="/wiki/Orbital_overlap" title="Orbital overlap">orbital overlap</a> then determines if the interactions will be physisorptive or chemisorptive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McQuarrie97_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-McQuarrie97-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Adsorption of molecular hydrogen in MOFs is physisorptive. Since molecular hydrogen only has two electrons, dispersion forces are weak, typically 4-7 kJ/mol, and are only sufficient for adsorption at temperatures below 298 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray09_23-19"><a href="#cite_note-Murray09-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>A complete explanation of the H<sub>2</sub> sorption mechanism in MOFs was achieved by statistical averaging in the grand canonical ensemble, exploring a wide range of pressures and temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belof_socMOF_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belof_socMOF-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belof_MOF5_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belof_MOF5-52">[52]</a></sup> Later work  revealed a pore filling mechanism as important in both COFs and MOFs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mendoza_COFs_MOFs_2012_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-mendoza_COFs_MOFs_2012-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Determining_hydrogen_storage_capacity">Determining hydrogen storage capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Determining hydrogen storage capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>
Two hydrogen-uptake measurement methods are used for the characterization of MOFs as hydrogen storage materials: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravimetric" title="Gravimetric">gravimetric</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volumetric" title="Volumetric">volumetric</a>. To obtain the total amount of hydrogen in the MOF, both the amount of hydrogen absorbed on its surface and the amount of hydrogen residing in its pores should be considered. To calculate the absolute absorbed amount (<i>N</i><sub>abs</sub>), the surface excess amount (<i>N</i><sub>ex</sub>) is added to the product of the bulk density of hydrogen (ρ<sub>bulk</sub>) and the pore volume of the MOF (<i>V</i><sub>pore</sub>), as shown in the following equation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao08_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao08-53">[53]</a></sup> </p><div style="text-align: center;"> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle N_{\rm {abs}}=N_{\rm {ex}}+\rho _{\rm {bulk}}V_{\rm {pore}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>N</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravimetric_method">Gravimetric method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Gravimetric method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The increased mass of the MOF due to the stored hydrogen is directly calculated by a highly sensitive microbalance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao08_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao08-53">[53]</a></sup> Due to <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyancy</a>, the detected mass of adsorbed hydrogen decreases again when a sufficiently high pressure is applied to the system because the density of the surrounding gaseous hydrogen becomes more and more important at higher pressures. Thus, this "weight loss" has to be corrected using the volume of the MOF’s frame and the density of hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Furukawa07_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Furukawa07-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Volumetric_method">Volumetric method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Volumetric method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The changing of amount of hydrogen stored in the MOF is measured by detecting the varied pressure of hydrogen at constant volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao08_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao08-53">[53]</a></sup> The volume of adsorbed hydrogen in the MOF is then calculated by subtracting the volume of hydrogen in free space from the total volume of dosed hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowell04_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowell04-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_methods_of_hydrogen_storage">Other methods of hydrogen storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other methods of hydrogen storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are six possible methods that can be used for the reversible storage of hydrogen with a high volumetric and gravimetric density, which are summarized in the following table, (where ρ<sub>m</sub> is the gravimetric density, ρ<sub>v</sub> is the volumetric density, <i>T</i> is the working temperature, and <i>P</i> is the working pressure):<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuttel04_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zuttel04-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; margin: 1em auto 1em auto">
<tbody><tr>
<th style="width:20%;">Storage method</th>
<th style="width:15%;">ρ<sub>m</sub> (mass%)</th>
<th style="width:15%;">ρ<sub>v</sub> (kg H<sub>2</sub>/m<sup>3</sup>)</th>
<th style="width:10%;"><i>T</i> (°C)</th>
<th style="width:10%;"><i>P</i> (bar)</th>
<th style="width:30%;">Remarks
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>High-pressure gas cylinders</td>
<td>13</td>
<td>&lt;40</td>
<td>25</td>
