<p>Of these, high-pressure gas cylinders and liquid hydrogen in cryogenic tanks are the least practical ways to store hydrogen for the purpose of fuel due to the extremely <i>high</i> pressure required for storing hydrogen gas or the extremely <i>low</i> temperature required for storing hydrogen liquid. The other methods are all being studied and developed extensively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zuttel04_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zuttel04-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Catalysis">Catalysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Catalysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>MOFs have potential as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterogeneous_catalyst" title="Heterogeneous catalyst">heterogeneous catalysts</a>, although applications have not been commercialized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>  Their high surface area, tunable porosity, diversity in metal and functional groups make them especially attractive for use as catalysts. Zeolites are extraordinarily useful in catalysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CejkaCorma2010_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-CejkaCorma2010-58">[58]</a></sup> Zeolites are limited by the fixed tetrahedral coordination of the Si/Al connecting points and the two-coordinated oxide linkers.  Fewer than 200 zeolites are known.  In contrast with this limited scope, MOFs exhibit more diverse <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordination_chemistry" title="Coordination chemistry">coordination geometries</a>, polytopic linkers, and ancillary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ligands" title="Ligands">ligands</a> (F<sup>−</sup>, OH<sup>−</sup>, H<sub>2</sub>O among others). It is also difficult to obtain zeolites with pore sizes larger than 1 nm, which limits the catalytic applications of zeolites to relatively small organic molecules (typically no larger than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xylenes" title="Xylenes">xylenes</a>). Furthermore, mild synthetic conditions typically employed for MOF synthesis allow direct incorporation of delicate functionalities into the framework structures. Such a process would not be possible with zeolites or other microporous crystalline oxide-based materials because of the harsh conditions typically used for their synthesis (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Calcination" title="Calcination">calcination</a> at high temperatures to remove organic templates).
</p><p>Zeolites still cannot be obtained in enantiopure form, which precludes their applications in catalytic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asymmetric_synthesis" title="Asymmetric synthesis">asymmetric synthesis</a>, e.g., for the pharmaceutical, agrochemical, and fragrance industries. Enantiopure chiral ligands or their metal complexes have been incorporated into MOFs to lead to efficient asymmetric catalysts. Even some MOF materials may bridge the gap between zeolites and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzymes" title="Enzymes">enzymes</a> when they combine isolated polynuclear sites, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/dynamic" title="wikt:dynamic">dynamic</a> host-guest responses, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> cavity environment. MOFs might be useful for making semi-conductors. Theoretical calculations show that MOFs are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiconductors" title="Semiconductors">semiconductors</a> or <a href="/wiki/Insulator_(electricity)" title="Insulator (electricity)">insulators</a> with band gaps between 1.0 and 5.5 eV which can be altered by changing the degree of <a href="/wiki/Conjugated_system" title="Conjugated system">conjugation</a> in the ligands indicating its possibility for being photocatalysts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IRMOF-1_wiki.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="740" data-file-width="744" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/IRMOF-1_wiki.png/220px-IRMOF-1_wiki.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/IRMOF-1_wiki.png/330px-IRMOF-1_wiki.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/IRMOF-1_wiki.png/440px-IRMOF-1_wiki.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IRMOF-1_wiki.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of MOF-5</div></div></div>
<p>Like other <a href="/wiki/Heterogeneous_catalysis" title="Heterogeneous catalysis">heterogeneous catalysts</a>, MOFs may allow for easier post-reaction separation and recyclability than <a href="/wiki/Homogeneous_catalysis" title="Homogeneous catalysis">homogeneous catalysts</a>. In some cases, they also give a highly enhanced catalyst stability. Additionally, they typically offer substrate-size selectivity. Nevertheless, while clearly important for reactions in living systems, selectivity on the basis of substrate size is of limited value in abiotic catalysis, as reasonably pure feedstocks are generally available.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_ions_or_metal_clusters">Metal ions or metal clusters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Metal ions or metal clusters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1420" data-file-width="1876" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/220px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/330px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png/440px-Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zeolite-ZSM-5-vdW.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Example of zeolite catalyst</div></div></div>
<p>Among the earliest reports of MOF-based catalysis was the cyanosilylation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aldehydes" title="Aldehydes">aldehydes</a> by a 2D MOF (layered square grids) of formula Cd(4,4’-bpy)<sub>2</sub>(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This investigation centered mainly on size- and shape-selective clathration.  A second set of examples was based on a two-dimensional, square-grid MOF containing single <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">Pd</a>(II) ions as nodes and 2-hydroxypyrimidinolates as struts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Despite initial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinative_saturation" title="Coordinative saturation">coordinative saturation</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">palladium</a> centers in this MOF catalyze alcohol oxidation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olefin" title="Olefin">olefin</a> hydrogenation, and Suzuki C–C coupling. At a minimum, these reactions necessarily entail redox oscillations of the metal nodes between <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">Pd</a>(II) and <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">Pd</a>(0) intermediates accompanying by drastic changes in coordination number, which would certainly lead to destabilization and potential destruction of the original framework if all the <a href="/wiki/Palladium" title="Palladium">Pd</a> centers are catalytically active. The observation of substrate shape- and size-selectivity implies that the catalytic reactions are heterogeneous and are indeed occurring within the MOF. Nevertheless, at least for hydrogenation, it is difficult to rule out the possibility that catalysis is occurring at the surface of MOF-encapsulated palladium clusters/nanoparticles (i.e., partial decomposition sites) or defect sites, rather than at transiently labile, but otherwise intact, single-atom MOF nodes.  "Opportunistic" MOF-based catalysis has been described for the cubic compound, MOF-5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> This material comprises coordinatively saturated Zn<sub>4</sub>O nodes and a fully complexed BDC struts (see above for abbreviation); yet it apparently catalyzes the Friedel–Crafts tert-butylation of both <a href="/wiki/Toluene" title="Toluene">toluene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biphenyl" title="Biphenyl">biphenyl</a>. Furthermore, para <a href="/wiki/Alkylation" title="Alkylation">alkylation</a> is strongly favored over ortho alkylation, a behavior thought to reflect the encapsulation of reactants by the MOF.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Functional_struts">Functional struts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Functional struts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The porous-framework material [Cu<sub>3</sub>(btc)<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub>], also known as HKUST-1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> contains large cavities having windows of diameter ~6 Å. The coordinated water molecules are easily removed, leaving open Cu(II) sites. Kaskel and co-workers showed that these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a> sites could catalyze the cyanosilylation of <a href="/wiki/Benzaldehyde" title="Benzaldehyde">benzaldehyde</a> or <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>.  The anhydrous version of HKUST-1 is an acid catalyst.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Compared to <a href="/wiki/Br%C3%B8nsted%E2%80%93Lowry_acid%E2%80%93base_theory" title="Brønsted–Lowry acid–base theory">Brønsted</a> vs. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a>-catalyzed pathways, the product selectivity are distinctive for three reactions: isomerization of a-pinene oxide, cyclization of citronellal, and rearrangement of a-bromoacetals, indicating that indeed [Cu<sub>3</sub>(btc)<sub>2</sub>] functions primarily as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>.
</p><p>MIL-101, a large-cavity MOF having the formula [Cr<sub>3</sub>F(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>O(BDC)<sub>3</sub>], is a cyanosilylation catalyst.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The coordinated water molecules in MIL-101 are easily removed to expose Cr(III) sites. As one might expect, given the greater Lewis acidity of Cr(III) vs. Cu(II), MIL-101 is much more active than HKUST-1 as a catalyst for the cyanosilylation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aldehydes" title="Aldehydes">aldehydes</a>. Additionally, the Kaskel group observed that the catalytic sites of MIL-101, in contrast to those of HKUST-1, are immune to unwanted reduction by <a href="/wiki/Benzaldehyde" title="Benzaldehyde">benzaldehyde</a>. The Lewis-acid-catalyzed cyanosilylation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aromatic" title="Aromatic">aromatic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aldehydes" title="Aldehydes">aldehydes</a> has also been carried out by Long and co-workers using a MOF of the formula Mn<sub>3</sub>[(Mn<sub>4</sub>Cl)<sub>3</sub>BTT<sub>8</sub>(CH<sub>3</sub>OH)<sub>10</sub>].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> This material contains a three-dimensional pore structure, with the pore diameter equaling 10 Å. In principle, either of the two types of <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">Mn</a>(II) sites could function as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. Noteworthy features of this catalyst are high conversion yields (for small substrates) and good substrate-size-selectivity, consistent with channellocalized catalysis.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Encapsulated_catalysts">Encapsulated catalysts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Encapsulated catalysts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The MOF encapsulation approach invites comparison to earlier studies of oxidative catalysis by zeolite-encapsulated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_(element)" title="Iron (element)">Fe</a>(<a href="/wiki/Porphyrin" title="Porphyrin">porphyrin</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">Mn</a>(<a href="/wiki/Porphyrin" title="Porphyrin">porphyrin</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> systems. The <a href="/wiki/Zeolite" title="Zeolite">zeolite</a> studies generally employed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iodosylbenzene" title="Iodosylbenzene">iodosylbenzene</a> (PhIO), rather than TPHP as oxidant. The difference is likely mechanistically significant, thus complicating comparisons. Briefly, PhIO is a single oxygen atom donor, while TBHP is capable of more complex behavior. In addition, for the MOF-based system, it is conceivable that oxidation proceeds via both oxygen transfer from a <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxo_ligand" title="Oxo ligand">oxo</a> intermediate as well as a <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>-initiated radical chain reaction pathway. Regardless of mechanism, the approach is a promising one for isolating and thereby stabilizing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porphyrins" title="Porphyrins">porphyrins</a> against both oxo-bridged <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimer</a> formation and oxidative degradation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal-free_organic_cavity_modifiers">Metal-free organic cavity modifiers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Metal-free organic cavity modifiers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most examples of MOF-based <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a> make use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_ions" title="Metal ions">metal ions</a> or atoms as active sites. Among the few exceptions are two <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>- and two <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>-containing MOFs synthesized by Rosseinsky and co-workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> These compounds employ <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acids" title="Amino acids">amino acids</a>(L- or D-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspartate" title="Aspartate">aspartate</a>) together with dipyridyls as struts. The <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination</a> chemistry is such that the amine group of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspartate" title="Aspartate">aspartate</a> cannot be protonated by added <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HCl" title="HCl">HCl</a>, but one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspartate" title="Aspartate">aspartate</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboxylates" title="Carboxylates">carboxylates</a> can. Thus, the framework-incorporated <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> can exist in a form that is not accessible for the free <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a>. While the <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>-based compounds are marginally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porous" title="Porous">porous</a>, on account of tiny channel dimensions, the <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> versions are clearly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porous" title="Porous">porous</a>.
The Rosseinsky group showed that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acids" title="Carboxylic acids">carboxylic acids</a> behave as Brønsted acidic catalysts, facilitating (in the copper cases) the ring-opening methanolysis of a small, cavityaccessible <a href="/wiki/Epoxide" title="Epoxide">epoxide</a> at up to 65% yield. Superior homogeneous catalysts exist however.
<a href="/wiki/Susumu_Kitagawa" title="Susumu Kitagawa">Kitagawa</a> and co-workers have reported the synthesis of a catalytic MOF having the formula [Cd(4-BTAPA)<sub>2</sub>(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The MOF is three-dimensional, consisting of an identical catenated pair of networks, yet still featuring pores of molecular dimensions. The nodes consist of single <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> ions, octahedrally ligated by pyridyl nitrogens. From a <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysis</a> standpoint, however, the most interesting feature of this material is the presence of guest-accessible <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amide</a> functionalities. The amides are capable of base-catalyzing the Knoevenagel condensation of <a href="/wiki/Benzaldehyde" title="Benzaldehyde">benzaldehyde</a> with <a href="/wiki/Malononitrile" title="Malononitrile">malononitrile</a>. Reactions with larger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitriles" title="Nitriles">nitriles</a>, however, are only marginally accelerated, implying that catalysis takes place chiefly within the material’s channels rather than on its exterior. A noteworthy finding is the lack of catalysis by the free strut in homogeneous solution, evidently due to intermolecular H-bonding between bptda molecules. Thus, the MOF architecture elicits catalytic activity not otherwise encountered.
In an interesting alternative approach, <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_F%C3%A9rey" title="Gérard Férey">Férey</a> and coworkers were able to modify the interior of MIL-101 via <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">Cr</a>(III) coordination of one of the two available <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atoms" title="Atoms">atoms</a> of each of several <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine" title="Ethylenediamine">ethylenediamine</a> molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The free non-coordinated ends of the ethylenediamines were then used as Brønsted basic catalysts, again for <a href="/wiki/Knoevenagel_condensation" title="Knoevenagel condensation">Knoevenagel condensation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Benzaldehyde" title="Benzaldehyde">benzaldehyde</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitriles" title="Nitriles">nitriles</a>.
A third approach has been described by Kim and coworkers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Using a <a href="/wiki/Pyridine" title="Pyridine">pyridine</a>-functionalized derivative of tartaric acid and a <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">Zn</a>(II) source they were able to synthesize a 2D MOF termed POST-1. POST-1 possesses 1D channels whose cross sections are defined by six trinuclear <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> clusters and six struts. While three of the six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyridines" title="Pyridines">pyridines</a> are coordinated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zinc_ions" title="Zinc ions">zinc ions</a>, the remaining three are protonated and directed toward the channel interior. When neutralized, the noncoordinated pyridyl groups are found to catalyze <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a> reactions, presumably by facilitating <a href="/wiki/Deprotonation" title="Deprotonation">deprotonation</a> of the reactant <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>. The absence of significant catalysis when large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohols" title="Alcohols">alcohols</a> are employed strongly suggests that the catalysis occurs within the channels of the MOF.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Achiral_catalysis">Achiral catalysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Achiral catalysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MOFscat3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="752" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MOFscat3.jpg/220px-MOFscat3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MOFscat3.jpg/330px-MOFscat3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MOFscat3.jpg/440px-MOFscat3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MOFscat3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic Diagram for MOF Catalysis</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metals_as_catalytic_sites">Metals as catalytic sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Metals as catalytic sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The metals in the MOF structure often act as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acids" title="Lewis acids">Lewis acids</a>. The metals in MOFs often coordinate to labile solvent molecules or counter ions which can be removed after activation of the framework. The Lewis acidic nature of such unsaturated metal centers can activate the coordinated organic substrates for subsequent organic transformations. The use of unsaturated metal centers was demonstrated in the cyanosilylation of aldehydes and imines by Fujita and coworkers in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chemcom1_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chemcom1-73">[73]</a></sup> They reported MOF of composition {[Cd(4,4′-bpy)<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>] • (NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> • 4H<sub>2</sub>O} which was obtained by treating linear bridging ligand 4,4′-<a href="/wiki/Bipyridine" title="Bipyridine">bipyridine</a> (bpy) with Cd(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> . The Cd(II) centers in this MOF possesses a distorted octahedral geometry having four pyridines in the equatorial positions, and two water molecules in the axial positions to form a two-dimensional infinite network. On activation, two water molecules were removed leaving the metal centers unsaturated and Lewis acidic. The Lewis acidic character of metal center was tested on cyanosilylation reactions of <a href="/wiki/Imine" title="Imine">imine</a> where the imine gets attached to the Lewis-acidic metal centre resulting in higher electrophilicity of imines. For the cyanosilylation of imines, most of the reactions were complete within 1 h affording aminonitriles in quantitative yield. Kaskel and coworkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> carried out similar cyanosilylation reactions with coordinatively unsaturated metals in three-dimensional (3D) MOFs as heterogeneous catalysts. The 3D framework [Cu<sub>3</sub>(btc)<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub>] (btc: Benzene-1,3,5- tricarboxylate) (HKUST-1) used in this study was first reported by Williams <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The open framework of [Cu<sub>3</sub>(btc)<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>3</sub>] is built from dimeric cupric tetracarboxylate units (paddle-wheels) with aqua molecules coordinating to the axial positions and btc bridging ligands. The resulting framework after removal of two water molecules from axial positions possesses porous channel. This activated MOF catalyzes the trimethylcyanosilylation of <a href="/wiki/Benzaldehyde" title="Benzaldehyde">benzaldehydes</a> with a very low conversion (&lt;5% in 24 h) at 293 K. As the reaction temperature was raised to 313 K, a good conversion of 57% with a selectivity of 89% was obtained after 72 h. In comparison, less than 10% conversion was observed for the background reaction (without MOF) under the same conditions. But this strategy suffers from some problems like 1) the decomposition of the framework with increase of the reaction temperature due to the reduction of Cu(II) to Cu(I) by <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a>; 2) strong solvent inhibition effect; electron donating solvents such as THF competed with aldehydes for coordination to the Cu(II) sites, and no cyanosilylation product was observed in these solvents; 3) the framework instability in some organic solvents. Several other groups have also reported the use of metal centres in MOFs as catalysts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Again, electron-deficient nature of some metals and metal clusters makes the resulting MOFs efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> catalysts. Mori and coworkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> reported MOFs with Cu<sub>2</sub> paddle wheel units as heterogeneous catalysts for the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a>. The catalytic activity of the resulting MOF was examined by carrying out alcohol oxidation with H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as the oxidant. It also catalyzed the oxidation of primary alcohol, secondary alcohol and benzyl alcohols with high selectivity. Hill <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> have demonstrated the sulfoxidation of <a href="/wiki/Thioether" title="Thioether">thioethers</a> using an MOF based on vanadium-oxo cluster V<sub>6</sub>O<sub>13</sub> building units.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Functional_linkers_as_catalytic_sites">Functional linkers as catalytic sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Functional linkers as catalytic sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Functional linkers can be also utilized as catalytic sites. A 3D MOF {[Cd(4- BTAPA)<sub>2</sub>(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>] • 6H<sub>2</sub>O • 2DMF} (4-BTAPA=1,3,5-benzene tricarboxylic acid tris [N-(4-pyridyl)amide], <a href="/wiki/Dimethylformamide" title="Dimethylformamide">DMF</a>=N,N-dimethylformamide) constructed by tridentate amide linkers and cadmium salt catalyzes the <a href="/wiki/Knoevenagel_condensation" title="Knoevenagel condensation">Knoevenagel condensation</a> reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacs2_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacs2-80">[80]</a></sup> The pyridine groups on the ligand 4-BTAPA act as ligands binding to the octahedral cadmium centers, while the amide groups can provide the functionality for interaction with the incoming substrates. Specifically, the – NH moiety of the amide group can act as <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a> acceptor whereas the C=O group can act as electron donor to activate organic substrates for subsequent reactions. Ferey <i>et al.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> reported a robust and highly porous MOF [Cr<sub>3</sub>(µ<sub>3</sub>-O)F(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>(BDC)<sub>3</sub>] (BDC: Benzene-1,4- dicarboxylate) where instead of directly using the unsaturated Cr(III) centers as catalytic sites, the authors grafted <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine" title="Ethylenediamine">ethylenediamine</a> (ED) onto the Cr(III) sites. The uncoordinated ends of ED can act as base catalytic sites, ED-grafted MOF was investigated for Knoevenagel condensation reactions. A significant increase in conversion was observed for ED-grafted MOF compared to untreated framework (98% vs 36%).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Entrapment_of_catalytically_active_noble_metal_nanoparticles">Entrapment of catalytically active noble metal nanoparticles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Entrapment of catalytically active noble metal nanoparticles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The entrapment of catalytically active <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noble_metals" title="Noble metals">noble metals</a> can be accomplished by grafting on functional groups to the unsaturated metal site on MOFs. Ethylenediamine (ED) has been shown to be grafted on the Cr metal sites and can be further modified to encapsulate noble metals such as Pd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angewandte1_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-angewandte1-82">[82]</a></sup> The entraped Pd has similar catalytic activity as Pd/C in the <a href="/wiki/Heck_reaction" title="Heck reaction">Heck reaction</a>. Ruthenium nanoparticles have catalytic activity in a number of reactions when entrapped in the MOF-5 framework.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> This Ru-encapsulated MOF catalyzes oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Benzyl_alcohol" title="Benzyl alcohol">benzyl alcohol</a> to benzyaldehyde, although degradation of the MOF occurs.  The same catalyst was used in the hydrogenation of <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexane" title="Cyclohexane">cyclohexane</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction_hosts_with_size_selectivity">Reaction hosts with size selectivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Reaction hosts with size selectivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>MOFs might prove useful for both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical" title="Photochemical">photochemical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> reactions due to the tuneability of the size and shape of their pores. A 3D MOF {[Co(bpdc)<sub>3</sub>(bpy)] • 4DMF • H<sub>2</sub>O} (bpdc: biphenyldicarboxylate, bpy: 4,4′-bipyridine) was synthesized by Li and coworkers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agnew2_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-agnew2-84">[84]</a></sup> Using this MOF photochemistry of <i>o</i>-methyl dibenzyl ketone (<i>o</i>-MeDBK) was extensively studied. This molecule was found to have a variety of photochemical reaction properties including the production of <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentanol" title="Cyclopentanol">cyclopentanol</a>. MOFs have been used to study polymerization in the confined space of MOF channels. Polymerization reactions in confined space might have different properties than polymerization in open space. <a href="/wiki/Styrene" title="Styrene">Styrene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Divinylbenzene" title="Divinylbenzene">divinylbenzene</a>, substituted <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylenes</a>, methyl methacrylate, and vinyl acetate have all been studied by <a href="/wiki/Susumu_Kitagawa" title="Susumu Kitagawa">Kitagawa</a> and coworkers as possible activated monomers for <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radical</a> polymerization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agnew3_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-agnew3-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agnew4_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-agnew4-86">[86]</a></sup> Due to the different linker size the MOF channel size could be tunable on the order of roughly 25 and 100 Å<sup>2</sup>. The channels were shown to stabilize propagating radicals and suppress termination reactions when used as radical polymerization sites.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asymmetric_catalysis">Asymmetric catalysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Asymmetric catalysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several strategies exist for constructing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homochiral" title="Homochiral">homochiral</a> MOFs. Crystallization of homochiral MOFs via self-resolution from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achiral" title="Achiral">achiral</a> linker ligands is one of the way to accomplish such a goal. However, the resulting bulk samples contain both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enantiomorph" title="Enantiomorph">enantiomorphs</a> and are racemic. Aoyama and coworkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> successfully obtained homochiral MOFs in the bulk from achiral ligands by carefully controlling nucleation in the <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystal</a> growth process. Zheng and coworkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> reported the synthesis of homochiral MOFs from achiral ligands by chemically manipulating the statistical fluctuation of the formation of enantiomeric pairs of crystals. Growing MOF crystals under chiral influences is another approach to obtain homochiral MOFs using achiral linker ligands. Rosseinsky and coworkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> have introduced a chiral coligand to direct the formation of homochiral MOFs by controlling the <a href="/wiki/Handedness" title="Handedness">handedness</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helice" title="Helice">helices</a> during the crystal growth. Morris and coworkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> utilized <a href="/wiki/Ionic_liquid" title="Ionic liquid">ionic liquid</a> with chiral cations as reaction media for synthesizing MOFs, and obtained homochiral MOFs. The most straightforward and rational strategy for synthesizing homochiral MOFs is, however, to use the readily available chiral linker ligands for their construction.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Homochiral_MOFs_with_interesting_functionalities_and_reagent-accessible_channels">Homochiral MOFs with interesting functionalities and reagent-accessible channels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Homochiral MOFs with interesting functionalities and reagent-accessible channels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Homochiral MOFs have been made by Lin and coworkers using 2,2′-bis(diphenylphosphino)-1,1′-binaphthyl (<a href="/wiki/BINAP" title="BINAP">BINAP</a>), 1,1′-bi-2,2′-naphthol (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BINOL" title="BINOL">BINOL</a>) as a chiral ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Angew4_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Angew4-92">[92]</a></sup> These ligands can coordinate with catalytically active metal sites to enhance the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enantioselectivity" title="Enantioselectivity">enantioselectivity</a>. A variety of linking groups such as <a href="/wiki/Pyridine" title="Pyridine">pyridine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphonic_acid" title="Phosphonic acid">phosphonic acid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carboxylic_acid" title="Carboxylic acid">carboxylic acid</a> can be selectively introduced to the 3,3′, 4,4′, and the 6,6′ positions of the 1,1'-binaphthyl moiety. Moreover, by changing the length of the linker ligands the porosity and framework structure of the MOF can be selectivily tuned.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Postmodification_of_homochiral_MOFs">Postmodification of homochiral MOFs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Postmodification of homochiral MOFs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Lin and coworkers have shown that the postmodification of MOFs can be achieved to produce enantioselective homochiral MOFs for use as catalysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JACS5_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-JACS5-93">[93]</a></sup> The resulting 3D homochiral MOF {[Cd<sub>3</sub>(L)<sub>3</sub>Cl<sub>6</sub>]• 4DMF • 6MeOH • 3H<sub>2</sub>O} (L=(R)-6,6'-dichloro-2,2'-dihydroxyl-1,1'-binaphthyl-bipyridine) synthesized by Lin was shown to have a similar catalytic efficiency for the diethylzinc addition reaction as compared to the homogeneous analogue when was pretreated by Ti(O<sup>i</sup>Pr)<sub>4</sub> to generate the grafted Ti- BINOLate species. The catalytic activity of MOFs can vary depending on the framework structure. Lin and others found that MOFs synthesized from the same materials could have drastically different catalytic activities depending on the framework structure present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Homochiral_MOFs_with_precatalysts_as_building_blocks">Homochiral MOFs with precatalysts as building blocks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Homochiral MOFs with precatalysts as building blocks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another approach to construct catalytically active homochiral MOFs is to incorporate chiral metal complexes which are either active catalysts or precatalysts directly into the framework structures. For example, Hupp and coworkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> have combined a chiral ligand and bpdc (bpdc: biphenyldicarboxylate) with Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> and obtained twofold interpenetrating 3D networks. The orientation of chiral ligand in the frameworks makes all Mn(III) sites accessible through the channels. The resulting open frameworks showed catalytic activity towards asymmetric olefin epoxidation reactions. No significant decrease of catalyst activity was observed during the reaction and the catalyst could be recycled and reused several times. Lin and coworkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> have reported zirconium phosphonate-derived Ru-BINAP systems. <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">Zirconium</a> phosphonate-based chiral porous hybrid materials containing the Ru(BINAP)(diamine)Cl<sub>2</sub> precatalysts showed excellent enantioselectivity (up to 99.2% ee) in the asymmetric hydrogenation of aromatic ketones.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biomimetic_design_and_photocatalysis">Biomimetic design and photocatalysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Biomimetic design and photocatalysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some MOF materials may resemble enzymes when they combine isolated polynuclear sites, dynamic host-guest responses, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> cavity environment which are characteristics of an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Some well-known examples of cooperative catalysis involving two metal ions in biological systems include: the diiron sites in <a href="/wiki/Methane_monooxygenase" title="Methane monooxygenase">methane monooxygenase</a>, dicopper in <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a>, and tricopper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidases" title="Oxidases">oxidases</a> which have analogy with polynuclear clusters found in the 0D coordination polymers, such as binuclear Cu<sub>2</sub> paddlewheel units found in MOP-1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> and [Cu<sub>3</sub>(btc)<sub>2</sub>] (btc=benzene-1,3,5-tricarboxylate) in HKUST-1 or trinuclear units such as {Fe<sub>3</sub>O(CO<sub>2</sub>)<sub>6</sub>} in MIL-88,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and IRMOP-51.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Thus, 0D MOFs have accessible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomimetic" title="Biomimetic">biomimetic</a> catalytic centers. In enzymatic systems, protein units show "molecular recognition", high affinity for specific substrates. It seems that molecular recognition effects are limited in zeolites by the rigid zeolite structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In contrast, dynamic features and guest-shape response make MOFs more similar to enzymes. Indeed, many hybrid frameworks contain organic parts that can rotate as a result of stimuli, such as light and heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The porous channels in MOF structures can be used as <a href="/wiki/Photocatalysis" title="Photocatalysis">photocatalysis</a> sites. In photocatalysis, the use of mononuclear complexes is usually limited either because they only undergo single- electron process or from the need for high-energy irradiation. In this case, binuclear systems have a number of attractive features for the development of photocatalysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> For 0D MOF structures, polycationic nodes can act as semiconductor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_dots" title="Quantum dots">quantum dots</a> which can be activated upon photo<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stimul" title="Stimul">stimuli</a> with the linkers serving as photon antennae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Theoretical calculations show that MOFs are <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a> or <a href="/wiki/Insulator_(electricity)" title="Insulator (electricity)">insulators</a> with band gaps between 1.0 and 5.5 eV which can be altered by changing the degree of conjugation in the ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Experimental results show that the <a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">band gap</a> of IRMOF-type samples can be tuned by varying the functionality of the linker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_potential_applications">Additional potential applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Additional potential applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><b><big>Biological imaging and sensing</big></b>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MOF-76.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="670" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/MOF-76.jpg/220px-MOF-76.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/MOF-76.jpg/330px-MOF-76.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/MOF-76.jpg/440px-MOF-76.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MOF-76.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>MOF-76 crystal, where oxygen, carbon, and lanthanide atoms are represented by maroon, black, and blue spheres, respectively. Includes metal node connectivity (blue polyhedra), infinite-rod SBU, and 3D representation of MOF-76.</div></div></div>
<p>A potential application for MOFs is biological imaging and sensing via photoluminescence. A large subset of luminescent MOFs use lanthanides in the metal clusters. <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide_probes" title="Lanthanide probes">Lanthanide photoluminescence</a> has many unique properties that make them ideal for imaging applications, such as characteristically sharp and generally non-overlapping emission bands in the visible and near-infrared (NIR) regions of the spectrum, resistance to photobleaching or ‘blinking’, and long luminescence lifetimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> However, lanthanide emissions are difficult to sensitize directly because they must undergo <a href="/wiki/Laporte_rule" title="Laporte rule">LaPorte</a> forbidden f-f transitions. Indirect sensitization of lanthanide emission can be accomplished by employing the “antenna effect,” where the organic linkers act as antennae and absorb the excitation energy, transfer the energy to the excited state of the lanthanide, and yield lanthanide luminescence upon relaxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> A prime example of the antenna effect is demonstrated by MOF-76, which combines trivalent lanthanide ions and <a href="/wiki/Trimesic_acid" title="Trimesic acid">1,3,5-benzenetricarboxylate (BTC)</a> linkers to form infinite rod SBUs coordinated into a three dimensional lattice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> As demonstrated by multiple research groups, the BTC linker can effectively sensitize the lanthanide emission, resulting in a MOF with variable emission wavelengths depending on the lanthanide identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Additionally, the Yan group has shown that Eu<sup>3+</sup>- and Tb<sup>3+</sup>- MOF-76 can be used for selective detection of acetophenone from other volatile monoaromatic hydrocarbons. Upon acetophenone uptake, the MOF shows a very sharp decrease, or <a href="/wiki/Quenching_(fluorescence)" title="Quenching (fluorescence)">quenching</a>, of the luminescence intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>For use in biological imaging, however, two main obstacles must be overcome: 
</p>
<ul><li>MOFs must be synthesized on the nanoscale so as not to affect the target’s normal interactions or behavior</li>
<li>The absorbance and emission wavelengths must occur in regions with minimal overlap from sample autofluorescence, other absorbing species, and maximum tissue penetration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Regarding the first point, nanoscale MOF (NMOF) synthesis has been mentioned in an earlier section. The latter obstacle addresses the limitation of the antenna effect. Smaller linkers tend to improve MOF stability, but have higher energy absorptions, predominantly in the ultraviolet (UV) and high-energy visible regions. A design strategy for MOFs with <a href="/wiki/Bathochromic_shift" title="Bathochromic shift">redshifted</a> absorption properties has been accomplished by using large, chromophoric linkers. These linkers are often composed of polyaromatic species, leading to large pore sizes and thus decreased stability. To circumvent the use of large linkers, other methods are required to redshift the absorbance of the MOF so lower energy excitation sources can be used. Post-synthetic modification (PSM) is one promising strategy. Luo et al. introduced a new family of lanthanide MOFs with functionalized organic linkers. The MOFs, deemed MOF-1114, MOF-1115, MOF-1130, and MOF-1131, are composed of octahedral SBUs bridged by amino functionalized dicarboxylate linkers. The amino groups on the linkers served as sites for covalent PSM reactions with either salicylaldehyde or 3-hydroxynaphthalene-2-carboxyaldehyde. Both of these reactions extend the π-conjugation of the linker, causing a redshift in the absorbance wavelength from 450 nm to 650 nm. The authors also propose that this technique could be adapted to similar MOF systems and, by increasing pore volumes with increasing linker lengths, larger pi-conjugated reactants can be used to further redshift the absorption wavelengths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Biological imaging using MOFs has been realized by several groups, namely Foucault-Collet and co-workers. In 2013, they synthesized a NIR-emitting Yb<sup>3+</sup>-NMOF using phenylenevinylene dicarboxylate (PVDC) linkers. They were observed cellular uptake in both HeLa cells and NIH-3T3 cells using confocal, visible, and NIR spectroscopy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Although low quantum yields persist in water and Hepes buffer solution, the luminescence intensity is still strong enough to image cellular uptake in both the visible and NIR regimes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drug_delivery_systems">Drug delivery systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Drug delivery systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The synthesis, characterization, and drug-related studies of low toxicity, biocompatible MOFs has shown that they have potential for medical applications. Many groups have synthesized various low toxicity MOFs and have studied their uses in loading and releasing various therapeutic drugs for potential medical applications. A variety of methods exist for inducing drug release, such as pH-response, magnetic-response, ion-response, temperature-response, and pressure response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010 Smaldone et. al, an international research group, synthesized a biocompatible MOF termed CD-MOF-1 from cheap edible natural products. CD-MOF-1 consists of repeating base units of 6 γ-cyclodextrin rings bound together by potassium ions. γ-cyclodextrin (γ-CD) is a symmetrical cyclic oligosaccharide that is mass-produced enzymatically from starch and consists of eight asymmetric α-1,4-linked D-glucopyranosyl residues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The molecular structure of these glucose derivatives, which approximates a truncated cone, bucket, or torus, generates a hydrophilic exterior surface and a nonpolar interior cavity. Cyclodextrins can interact with appropriately sized drug molecules to yield an inclusion complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Smaldone’s group proposed a cheap and simple synthesis of the CD-MOF-1 from natural products. They dissolved sugar (γ-cyclodextrin) and an alkali salt (KOH, KCl, potassium benzoate) in distilled bottled water and allowed 190 proof grain alcohol (Everclear) to vapor diffuse into the solution for a week. The synthesis resulted in a cubic (γ-CD)<small>6</small> repeating motif with a pore size of approximately 1nm. Subsequently, in 2017 Hartlieb et. al. at Northwestern did further research with CD-MOF-1 involving the encapsulation of ibuprofen. The group studied different methods of loading the MOF with ibuprofen as well as performing related bioavailability studies on the ibuprofen-loaded MOF. They investigated two different methods of loading CD-MOF-1 with ibuprofen; crystallization using the potassium salt of ibuprofen as the alkali cation source for production of the MOF, and absorption and deprotonation of the free-acid of ibuprofen into the MOF. From there the group performed in vitro and in vivo studies to determine the applicability of CD-MOF-1 as a viable delivery method for ibuprofen and other NSAIDs. In vitro studies showed no toxicity or effect on cell viability up to 100 μM. In vivo studies in mice showed the same rapid uptake of ibuprofen as the ibuprofen potassium salt control sample with a peak plasma concentration observed within 20 minutes, and the cocrystal has the added benefit of double the half-life in blood plasma samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> The increase in half-life is due to CD-MOF-1 increasing the solubility of ibuprofen compared to the pure salt form.
</p><p>Since these developments many groups have done further research into drug delivery with water soluble, biocompatible MOFs involving common over-the-counter drugs. In March of 2018 Sara Rojas and her team at Paris-Saclay University published their research on drug incorporation and delivery with various biocompatible MOFs other than CD-MOF-1 through simulated cutaneous administration. The group studied the loading and release of ibuprofen (hydrophobic) and aspirin (hydrophilic) in three biocompatible MOFs (MIL-100(Fe), UiO-66(Zr), and MIL-127(Fe)). Under simulated cutaneous conditions (aqueous media at 37 °C) the six different combinations of drug-loaded MOFs fulfilled “the requirements to be used as topical drug-delivery systems, such as released payload between 1 and 7 days” and delivering a therapeutic concentration of the drug of choice without causing unwanted side effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The group discovered that the drug uptake is “governed by the hydrophilic/hydrophobic balance between cargo and matrix” and “the accessibility of the drug through the framework.” The “controlled release under cutaneous conditions follows different kinetics profiles depending on: (i) the structure of the framework, with either a fast delivery from the very open structure MIL-100 or a slower drug release from the narrow 1D pore system of MIL-127 or (ii) the hydrophobic/hydrophilic nature of the cargo, with a fast (Aspirin) and slow (Ibuprofen) release from the UiO-66 matrix.”
</p><p>Recent research involving MOFs as a drug delivery method includes more than just the encapsulation of everyday drugs like ibuprofen and aspirin. In early 2018 Chen et. al, from Zhejiang University in Hangzhou China, released an article in ACS’s <i>Applied Materials and Interfaces</i> detailing their work on the use of MOF ZIF-8 (zeolitic imidazolate framework-8) in antitumor research “to control the release of an autophagy inhibitor, 3-methyladenine (3-MA), and prevent it from dissipating in a large quantity before reaching the target.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The group performed in vitro studies and determined that “the autophagy-related proteins and autophagy flux in HeLa cells treated with 3-MA@ZIF-8 NPs show that the autophagosome formation is significantly blocked, which reveals that the pH-sensitive dissociation increases the efficiency of autophagy inhibition at the equivalent concentration of 3-MA.” This shows promise for future research and applicability with MOFs as drug delivery methods in the fight against cancer.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane_storage">Methane storage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Methane storage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 2011, researchers at the California Institute of Technology synthesized 14 new Covalent-Organic Frameworks (COFs). Two of these COFs, COF-103-Eth-trans and COF-102-Ant, were found to exceed the DOE target of 180 v(STP)/v at 35 bar for methane storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPCA_Mendoza_2011_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPCA_Mendoza_2011-124">[124]</a></sup> They reported that using thin vinyl bridging groups aid performance by minimizing the interaction between methane and the COF at low pressure. It is believed that this method could be extended to MOFs due to the similarity between MOFs and COFs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPCA_Mendoza_2011_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-JPCA_Mendoza_2011-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Semiconductors">Semiconductors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Semiconductors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014 researchers proved that they can create electrically conductive thin films of MOFs (Cu<sub>3</sub>(BTC)<sub>2</sub> (also known as HKUST-1; BTC, benzene-1,3,5-tricarboxylic acid) infiltrated with the molecule 7,7,8,8-tetracyanoquinododimethane) that could be used in applications including photovoltaics, sensors and electronic materials and a path towards creating semiconductors. The team demonstrated tunable, air-stable electrical conductivity with values as high as 7 siemens per meter, comparable to bronze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="chemf nowrap">Ni<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>(2,3,6,7,10,11-hexaiminotriphenylene)<sub>2</sub> was shown to be a metal-organic <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a> analogue that has a natural <a href="/wiki/Band_gap" title="Band gap">band gap</a>, making it a semiconductor, and is able to self-assemble. It represents a family of similar compounds. Because of the symmetry and geometry in 2,3,6,7,10,11-hexaiminotriphenylene (HITP), the overall organometallic complex has an almost <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a> nature that allows it to perfectly self-organize. By contrast, graphene must be doped to give it the properties of a semiconductor. Ni<sub>3</sub>(HITP)<sub>2</sub> pellets had a conductivity of 2 S/cm, a record for a metal-organic compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bio-mimetic_mineralization">Bio-mimetic mineralization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Bio-mimetic mineralization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Biomolecules can be incorporated during the MOF crystallization process. Biomolecules including proteins, DNA and antibodies could be encapsulated within ZIF-8. Enzymes encapsulated in this way were stable and active even after being exposed to harsh conditions (e.g. aggressive solvents and high temperature). ZIF-8, MIL-88A, HKUST-1, and several luminescent MOFs containing lanthanide metals were used for the biomimetic mineralization process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_capture">Carbon capture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Carbon capture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">carbon capture and storage</a></div>
<p>Because of their small, tunable pore sizes and high void fractions, MOFs are a promising potential material for use as an adsorbent to capture CO<sub>2</sub>.  MOFs could provide a more efficient alternative to traditional <a href="/wiki/Amine_gas_treating" title="Amine gas treating">amine solvent-based methods</a> in CO<sub>2</sub> capture from coal-fired power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>MOFs could be employed in each of the main three carbon capture configurations for coal-fired power plants: pre-combustion, post-combusiton, and oxy-combustion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-130">[130]</a></sup> However, since the post-combustion configuration is the only one that can be retrofitted to existing plants, it garners the most interest and research. In post-combustion carbon capture, the flue gas from the power plant would be fed through a MOF in a packed-bed reactor setup. Flue gas is generally 40 to 60 °C with a partial pressure of CO<sub>2</sub> at 0.13 - 0.16 bar. CO<sub>2</sub> can bind to the MOF surface through either <a href="/wiki/Physisorption" title="Physisorption">physisorption</a>, which is caused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_interactions" title="Van der Waals interactions">Van der Waals interactions</a>, or  <a href="/wiki/Chemisorption" title="Chemisorption">chemisorption</a>, which is caused by <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bond</a> formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Once the MOF is saturated with CO<sub>2</sub>, the CO<sub>2</sub> would be removed from the MOF through either a temperature swing or a pressure swing. This process is known as regeneration. In a temperature swing regeneration, the MOF would be heated until CO<sub>2</sub> desorbs. To achieve working capacities comparable to the amine process, the MOF must be heated to around 200 °C. In a pressure swing, the pressure would be decreased until CO<sub>2</sub> desorbs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to their tunable selectivities for different molecules, another property of MOFs that makes them a good candidate for carbon capture is their low heat capacities.  Monoethanolamine (MEA) solutions, the leading method for capturing CO<sub>2</sub> from flue gas, have a heat capacity between 3-4 J/g K since they are mostly water.  This high heat capacity contributes to the energy penalty in the solvent regeneration step, i.e., when the adsorbed CO<sub>2</sub> is removed from the MEA solution.  MOF-177, a MOF designed for CO<sub>2</sub> capture, has a heat capacity of 0.5 J/g K at ambient temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a collaborative project sponsored by the U.S. DOE, MOFs were shown to separate 90% of the CO<sub>2</sub> from the flue gas stream using a vacuum pressure swing process. The MOF Mg(dobdc) has a 21.7 wt% CO<sub>2</sub> loading capacity. Estimations showed that, if a similar system would be applied to a large scale power plant, the cost of energy would increase by 65%, while a U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Energy_Technology_Laboratory" title="National Energy Technology Laboratory">NETL</a> baseline amine-based system would cause an increase of 81% (the U.S. DOE goal is 35%). The cost of capturing CO<sub>2</sub>  would be $57 / ton CO<sub>2</sub> captured, while for the amine system the cost is estimated to be $72 / ton CO<sub>2</sub> captured. The project estimated that the total capital required to implement such project in a 580 MW power plant would be $354 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Desalination.2Fion_separation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Desalination/ion_separation">Desalination/ion separation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Desalination/ion separation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>MOF membranes can mimic substantial ion selectivity. This offers the potential for use in desalination and water treatment. As of 2018 <a href="/wiki/Reverse_osmosis" title="Reverse osmosis">reverse osmosis</a> supplied more than half of global desalination capacity, and the last stage of most water treatment processes. Osmosis does not use <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a> of ions, or selective <a href="/wiki/Ion_transporter" title="Ion transporter">ion transport</a> in biological channels and it is not energy efficient. The mining industry, uses membrane-based processes to reduce water pollution, and to recover metals. MOFs  could be used to extract metals such as lithium from seawater and waste streams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>MOF membranes such as ZIF-8 and UiO-66 membranes with uniform subnanometer pores consisting of angstrom-scale windows and nanometer-scale cavities displayed ultrafast selective transport of alkali metal ions. The windows acted as ion selectivity filters for alkali metal ions, while the cavities functioned as pores for transport. The ZIF-8<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> and UiO-66<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> membranes showed a <a href="/wiki/Lithium_chloride" title="Lithium chloride">LiCl</a>/<a href="/wiki/Rubidium_chloride" title="Rubidium chloride">RbCl</a> selectivity of ~4.6 and ~1.8, respectively, much higher than the 0.6 to 0.8 selectivity in traditional membranes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_vapor_capture">Water vapor capture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Metal%E2%80%93organic_framework&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Water vapor capture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A team of researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_California_Berkeley" title="University of California Berkeley">University of California Berkeley</a> have developed a prototype that captures water vapor from the air, and then releases it with the application of a smaller amount of heat compared to existing commercially available technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
